<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en"><i>Dota 2</i></h1>			<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
				<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>				<div id="contentSub"></div>
				<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>				<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
				<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
				<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><p class="mw-empty-elt">

</p>
<table class="infobox hproduct" style="width:22em;float: right; width: 22em; font-size: 90%; text-align: left; border-spacing: 3px;"><tbody><tr><th colspan="2" class="fn" <p><i><b>Dota 2</b></i> is a <a href="/wiki/Multiplayer_online_battle_arena" title="Multiplayer online battle arena">multiplayer online battle arena</a> (MOBA) video game developed and published by <a href="/wiki/Valve_Corporation" title="Valve Corporation">Valve Corporation</a>. The game is a sequel to <i><a href="/wiki/Defense_of_the_Ancients" title="Defense of the Ancients">Defense of the Ancients</a></i> (<i>DotA</i>), which was a community-created <a href="/wiki/Mod_(video_gaming)" title="Mod (video gaming)">mod</a> for <a href="/wiki/Blizzard_Entertainment" title="Blizzard Entertainment">Blizzard Entertainment</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Warcraft_III:_Reign_of_Chaos" title="Warcraft III: Reign of Chaos">Warcraft III: Reign of Chaos</a></i> and its expansion pack, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Frozen_Throne" class="mw-redirect" title="The Frozen Throne">The Frozen Throne</a></i>. <i>Dota 2</i> is played in matches between two teams of five players, with each team occupying and defending their own separate base on the <a href="/wiki/Level_(video_gaming)" title="Level (video gaming)">map</a>. Each of the ten players independently controls a powerful character, known as a "hero", who all have unique <a href="/wiki/Skill_(role-playing_games)" class="mw-redirect" title="Skill (role-playing games)">abilities</a> and differing styles of play. During a match, players collect <a href="/wiki/Experience_point" title="Experience point">experience points</a> and <a href="/wiki/Item_(gaming)" title="Item (gaming)">items</a> for their heroes to successfully defeat the opposing team's heroes in <a href="/wiki/Player_versus_player" title="Player versus player">player versus player</a> combat. A team wins by being the first to destroy a large structure located in the opposing team's base, called the "Ancient".
</p><p>Development of <i>Dota 2</i> began in 2009 when <a href="/wiki/IceFrog" title="IceFrog">IceFrog</a>, lead designer of the original <i>Defense of the Ancients</i> mod, was hired by Valve to create a modernized remake for them in the <a href="/wiki/Source_game_engine" class="mw-redirect" title="Source game engine">Source game engine</a>. It was officially released for <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Windows" title="Microsoft Windows">Microsoft Windows</a>, <a href="/wiki/OS_X" class="mw-redirect" title="OS X">OS X</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Linux" title="Linux">Linux</a>-based personal computers via the digital distribution platform <a href="/wiki/Steam_(software)" title="Steam (software)">Steam</a> in July 2013, following a Windows-only <a href="/wiki/Public_beta" class="mw-redirect" title="Public beta">public beta</a> phase that began two years prior. As the game is fully <a href="/wiki/Free-to-play" title="Free-to-play">free-to-play</a> with no heroes needing to be bought or otherwise unlocked, revenue is made from <a href="/wiki/Microtransaction" title="Microtransaction">microtransactions</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Loot_box" title="Loot box">loot boxes</a>, and a <a href="/wiki/Battle_pass" title="Battle pass">battle pass</a> subscription system called Dota Plus, which all offer non-gameplay altering <a href="/wiki/Virtual_goods" title="Virtual goods">virtual goods</a> in return, such as cosmetic hero armor and weapon replacements. The game has also been updated with various other features since release, such as support for <a href="/wiki/Virtual_reality" title="Virtual reality">virtual reality</a> (VR) and a transition to the <a href="/wiki/Source_2" class="mw-redirect" title="Source 2">Source 2</a> engine in 2015.
</p><p>Despite some criticism going towards its steep <a href="/wiki/Learning_curve" title="Learning curve">learning curve</a> and complexity, <i>Dota 2</i> was praised for its rewarding gameplay, production quality, and faithfulness to its predecessor, with multiple gaming publications considering it to be one of the <a href="/wiki/Greatest_video_games_of_all_time" class="mw-redirect" title="Greatest video games of all time">greatest video games of all time</a>. Since its release, it has been one of the most played games on Steam, with over a million <a href="/wiki/Concurrent_players" class="mw-redirect" title="Concurrent players">concurrent players</a> at its peak. The popularity of the game has led to official merchandise for it being produced, including apparel, accessories, and toys, as well as promotional <a href="/wiki/Tie-in" title="Tie-in">tie-ins</a> to other games and media. The game also allows for the community to create custom game modes, maps, and hero cosmetics, which are uploaded to the <a href="/wiki/Steam_Workshop" class="mw-redirect" title="Steam Workshop">Steam Workshop</a> and curated by Valve. A <a href="/wiki/Digital_collectible_card_game" title="Digital collectible card game">digital collectible card game</a> featuring the characters and setting of <i>Dota 2</i>, called <i><a href="/wiki/Artifact_(video_game)" title="Artifact (video game)">Artifact</a></i>, was released in November 2018. Developed by Valve as well, the games will have content updates that are created in tandem with each other.
</p><p><i>Dota 2</i> has a widespread and active competitive scene, with teams from across the world playing professionally in various <a href="/wiki/Sports_league" title="Sports league">leagues</a> and tournaments. Premium tournaments of the game often have prize pools totaling millions of U.S. dollars, the highest of any <a href="/wiki/Esport" class="mw-redirect" title="Esport">esport</a>. The largest of them is known as <a href="/wiki/The_International_(Dota_2)" title="The International (Dota 2)">The International</a>, which is produced annually by Valve and most commonly held at the <a href="/wiki/KeyArena" title="KeyArena">KeyArena</a> in <a href="/wiki/Seattle" title="Seattle">Seattle</a>. Valve also manages an event format known as the <a href="/wiki/Dota_Pro_Circuit" title="Dota Pro Circuit">Dota Pro Circuit</a>, which are a series of tournaments held prior to Internationals that award qualification points based on results for getting directly invited to them. For most tournaments, media coverage is done by a selection of on-site staff who provide commentary and analysis for the ongoing matches, similar to traditional sporting events. Broadcasts of professional <i>Dota 2</i> matches are <a href="/wiki/Video_game_live_streaming" title="Video game live streaming">streamed live</a> over the internet, and sometimes simulcast on television networks, with peak viewership numbers in the millions. The game has also been used in <a href="/wiki/Machine_learning" title="Machine learning">machine learning</a> experiments, with a team of <a href="/wiki/Video_game_bot" title="Video game bot">bots</a> known as the <a href="/wiki/OpenAI_Five" title="OpenAI Five">OpenAI Five</a> showing the ability to compete against, and sometimes defeat, professional players.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Gameplay">Gameplay</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Multiplayer_online_battle_arena#Gameplay" title="Multiplayer online battle arena">Mechanics of multiplayer online battle arena games</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:327px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Dota_2_Gameplay_Aug_2017.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/5/53/Dota_2_Gameplay_Aug_2017.jpg/325px-Dota_2_Gameplay_Aug_2017.jpg" width="325" height="183" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/5/53/Dota_2_Gameplay_Aug_2017.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="421" data-file-height="237" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Dota_2_Gameplay_Aug_2017.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A game of <i>Dota 2</i> in progress, showing the Radiant team inside their base at the beginning of a match</div></div></div>
<p><i>Dota 2</i> is a <a href="/wiki/Multiplayer_online_battle_arena" title="Multiplayer online battle arena">multiplayer online battle arena</a> (MOBA) video game in which <a href="/wiki/Player_versus_player" title="Player versus player">two teams of five players</a> compete to collectively destroy a large structure defended by the opposing team known as the "Ancient", whilst defending their own.<sup id="cite_ref-Beginner&#39;s_Guide_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Beginner&#39;s_Guide-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-This_Is_Dota_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-This_Is_Dota-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> As in <i><a href="/wiki/Defense_of_the_Ancients" title="Defense of the Ancients">Defense of the Ancients</a></i>, the game is controlled using standard <a href="/wiki/Real-time_strategy#Gameplay" title="Real-time strategy">real-time strategy controls</a>, and is presented on a single <a href="/wiki/Level_(video_gaming)" title="Level (video gaming)">map</a> in a <a href="/wiki/3D_computer_graphics" title="3D computer graphics">three-dimensional</a> <a href="/wiki/Isometric_video_game_graphics#Similar_projections" title="Isometric video game graphics">isometric perspective</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Beginner&#39;s_Guide_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Beginner&#39;s_Guide-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Comparison_of_Dota_2_and_LoL_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Comparison_of_Dota_2_and_LoL-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> Ten players each control one of the game's 116 <a href="/wiki/Playable_character" class="mw-redirect" title="Playable character">playable characters</a>, known as "heroes", with each having their own design, strengths, and weaknesses.<sup id="cite_ref-Beginner&#39;s_Guide_1-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Beginner&#39;s_Guide-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-This_Is_Dota_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-This_Is_Dota-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> Heroes are divided into two primary <a href="/wiki/Character_class" title="Character class">roles</a>, known as the "carry" and "support".<sup id="cite_ref-Polygon_guide_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Polygon_guide-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-TFCguide_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TFCguide-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Carries, which are also called "cores", begin each match as weak and vulnerable, but are able to become more powerful later in the game, thus becoming able to "carry" their team to victory.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Polygon_guide_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Polygon_guide-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Supports generally lack abilities that deal heavy damage, instead having ones with more functionality and utility that provide assistance for their carries, such as providing healing and other <a href="/wiki/Buff_(video_gaming)" class="mw-redirect" title="Buff (video gaming)">buffs</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Heroes_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Heroes-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Polygon_guide_5-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Polygon_guide-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-TFCguide_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TFCguide-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Players select their hero during a pre-game drafting phase, where they can also discuss potential strategies and hero matchups with their teammates.<sup id="cite_ref-This_Is_Dota_2-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-This_Is_Dota-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Polygon_guide_5-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Polygon_guide-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-TFCguide_6-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TFCguide-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Heroes can not be switched mid-game, and once one is selected, they are removed from the drafting pool and become unavailable for all other players.
</p><p>All heroes have a basic damage-dealing attack, in addition to powerful <a href="/wiki/Skill_(role-playing_games)" class="mw-redirect" title="Skill (role-playing games)">abilities</a>. Each hero has at least four abilities, all of which are unique, which are the primary method of fighting.<sup id="cite_ref-Comparison_of_Dota_2_and_LoL_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Comparison_of_Dota_2_and_LoL-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Heroes_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Heroes-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> Heroes begin each game with an <a href="/wiki/Experience_point" title="Experience point">experience level</a> of one, only having access to one of their abilities, but are able to level up and become more powerful during the course of the game, up to a maximum level of 25.<sup id="cite_ref-This_Is_Dota_2-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-This_Is_Dota-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Polygon_guide_5-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Polygon_guide-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Whenever a hero gains an experience level, the player is able to unlock another of their abilities or improve one already learned.<sup id="cite_ref-This_Is_Dota_2-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-This_Is_Dota-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Polygon_guide_5-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Polygon_guide-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> The most powerful ability for each hero is known as their "ultimate", which requires them to have an experience level of six in order to use.<sup id="cite_ref-TFCguide_6-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TFCguide-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> In order to prevent abilities from being <a href="/wiki/Skill_spamming" class="mw-redirect" title="Skill spamming">used without consequence</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Magic_system" title="Magic system">magic system</a> in the game exists. Activating an ability costs a hero some of their "<a href="/wiki/Magic_(gaming)" title="Magic (gaming)">mana points</a>", which slowly regenerates over time.<sup id="cite_ref-Comparison_of_Dota_2_and_LoL_3-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Comparison_of_Dota_2_and_LoL-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Heroes_(continued)_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Heroes_(continued)-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Using an ability will also cause it to enter a <a href="/wiki/Cooldown" class="mw-redirect" title="Cooldown">cooldown</a> phase, in which the ability can not be used again until a timer counts down to zero. All heroes have three <a href="/wiki/Attribute_(role-playing_games)" title="Attribute (role-playing games)">attributes</a>: strength, intelligence, and agility, which affect <a href="/wiki/Health_(gaming)" title="Health (gaming)">health points</a>, mana points, and attack speed, respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-Polygon_guide_5-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Polygon_guide-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Each hero has one primary attribute out of the three, which adds to their non-ability basic damage output when increased.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Polygon_guide_5-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Polygon_guide-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Heroes also have an ability augmentation system known as "Talent Trees", which allow players further choices on how to develop their hero.<sup id="cite_ref-Polygon_guide_5-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Polygon_guide-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-TFCguide_6-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TFCguide-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> If a hero <a href="/wiki/Life_(gaming)" title="Life (gaming)">runs out of health points</a> and dies, they are removed from active play until a respawn timer counts down to zero, where they are then respawned in their base.<sup id="cite_ref-Gameplay_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gameplay-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The two teams—known as the Radiant and Dire—occupy fortified bases in opposite corners of the map, which is divided in half by a crossable river and connected by three paths, which are referred to as "lanes".<sup id="cite_ref-Beginner&#39;s_Guide_1-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Beginner&#39;s_Guide-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-This_Is_Dota_2-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-This_Is_Dota-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> The lanes are guarded by defensive towers that attack any opposing unit who gets within its <a href="/wiki/Line_of_sight_(gaming)" title="Line of sight (gaming)">line of sight</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-This_Is_Dota_2-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-This_Is_Dota-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> A small group of weak <a href="/wiki/Artificial_intelligence" title="Artificial intelligence">computer-controlled</a> creatures called "<a href="/wiki/Mob_(gaming)" title="Mob (gaming)">creeps</a>" travel predefined paths along the lanes and attempt to attack any opposing heroes, creeps, and buildings in their way.<sup id="cite_ref-This_Is_Dota_2-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-This_Is_Dota-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Gameplay_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gameplay-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-TFCguide_6-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TFCguide-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Creeps periodically spawn throughout the game in groups from two buildings, called the "barracks", that exist in each lane and are located within the team's bases.<sup id="cite_ref-This_Is_Dota_2-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-This_Is_Dota-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-TFCguide_6-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TFCguide-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> The map is also permanently covered for both teams in <a href="/wiki/Fog_of_war#In_video_games" title="Fog of war">fog of war</a>, which prevents a team from seeing the opposing team's heroes and creeps if they are not directly in sight of themselves or an allied unit. The map also features a day-night cycle, with some hero abilities and other game mechanics being altered depending on the time of the cycle.<sup id="cite_ref-Polygon_guide_5-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Polygon_guide-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Also present on the map are "neutral creeps" that are hostile to both teams, and reside in marked locations on the map known as "camps". Camps are located in the area between the lanes known as the "jungle", which both sides of the map have.<sup id="cite_ref-Dota_2_terms_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dota_2_terms-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-TFCguide_6-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TFCguide-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Neutral creeps do not attack unless provoked, and will respawn over time if killed. The most powerful neutral creep is named "Roshan", who is a unique <a href="/wiki/Boss_(video_gaming)" title="Boss (video gaming)">boss</a> that may be defeated by either team to obtain special <a href="/wiki/Item_(gaming)" title="Item (gaming)">items</a>, such as one that allows a one-time resurrection if the hero that holds it is killed.<sup id="cite_ref-This_Is_Dota_2-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-This_Is_Dota-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Basics_of_Dota_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Basics_of_Dota-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-TFCguide_6-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TFCguide-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Roshan will respawn around ten minutes after being killed, and becomes progressively harder to kill as the match progresses over time.<sup id="cite_ref-Basics_of_Dota_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Basics_of_Dota-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> "Runes", which are special items that spawn in set positions on the map every two minutes, offer heroes various temporary, but powerful <a href="/wiki/Power-ups" class="mw-redirect" title="Power-ups">power-ups</a> when collected.<sup id="cite_ref-Polygon_guide_5-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Polygon_guide-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-TFCguide_6-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TFCguide-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In addition to having abilities becoming stronger during the game, players are able to buy items from set locations on the map called shops that provide their own special abilities.<sup id="cite_ref-This_Is_Dota_2-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-This_Is_Dota-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Basics_of_Dota_14-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Basics_of_Dota-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-TFCguide_6-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TFCguide-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Items are not limited to specific heroes, and can be bought by anyone. In order to obtain an item, players must be able to afford it with gold at shops located on the map, which is primarily obtained by killing enemy heroes, destroying enemy structures, and killing creeps, with the latter being an act called "<a href="/wiki/Grinding_(gaming)" title="Grinding (gaming)">farming</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-This_Is_Dota_2-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-This_Is_Dota-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Gameplay_11-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gameplay-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Polygon_guide_5-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Polygon_guide-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Only the hero that lands the killing blow on a creep obtains gold from it, an act called "<a href="/wiki/Last_hitting" class="mw-redirect" title="Last hitting">last hitting</a>", but all allies receive a share of gold when an enemy hero dies close to them.<sup id="cite_ref-Comparison_of_Dota_2_and_LoL_3-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Comparison_of_Dota_2_and_LoL-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Gameplay_11-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gameplay-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Players are also able to "deny" allied units and structures by last hitting them, which then prevents their opponents from getting full experience from them.<sup id="cite_ref-Gameplay_11-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gameplay-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Basics_of_Dota_14-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Basics_of_Dota-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> Gold can not be shared between teammates, with each player having their own independent stash. Players also receive a continuous, but small stream of gold over the course of a match.<sup id="cite_ref-GIReview_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GIReview-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Polygon_guide_5-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Polygon_guide-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Multiple game types in the game exist, which mainly alter the way hero selection is handled; examples include "All Pick", which offer no restrictions on hero selection, "All Random", which randomly assigns a hero for each player, "Captain's Mode", where a single player on each team selects heroes for their entire team and is primarily used for professional play, and "Turbo", an expedited version of All Pick featuring increased gold and experience gain, weaker towers, and faster <a href="/wiki/Respawn" class="mw-redirect" title="Respawn">respawn</a> times.<sup id="cite_ref-Gamemodes_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gamemodes-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> Matches usually last around 30 minutes or more, although they can theoretically last forever as long as both Ancients remain standing.<sup id="cite_ref-TFCguide_6-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TFCguide-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> In Captain's Mode games, an additional "<a href="/wiki/GG_(gaming)" class="mw-redirect" title="GG (gaming)">GG</a>" forfeit feature is available to end games early.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><i>Dota 2</i> also occasionally features limited-time events that present players with alternative <a href="/wiki/Game_mode" class="mw-redirect" title="Game mode">game modes</a> that do not follow the game's standard rules.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> Some of these included the <a href="/wiki/Halloween" title="Halloween">Halloween</a>-themed Diretide event,<sup id="cite_ref-Diretide_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Diretide-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Christmas" title="Christmas">Christmas</a>-themed Frostivus event,<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> and the New Bloom Festival, which celebrated the coming of <a href="/wiki/Spring_(season)" title="Spring (season)">spring</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> The move to the Source 2 engine in 2015 also saw the addition of community-created custom game modes, known as the "Arcade", with the more popular ones having <a href="/wiki/Dedicated_server" class="mw-redirect" title="Dedicated server">dedicated server</a> hosting by Valve.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> Various games in the Arcade have also been created by Valve themselves, including the <a href="/wiki/Halloween" title="Halloween">Halloween</a>-themed capture point mode "Colosseum", the combat arena mode "Overthrow", and a 10 versus 10 mode.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> Starting in 2017, Valve began releasing special seasonal gamemodes as a part of each year's International <a href="/wiki/Battle_pass" title="Battle pass">battle pass</a>, such as "Siltbreaker", a story-driven <a href="/wiki/Cooperative_gameplay" title="Cooperative gameplay">cooperative campaign mode</a>, in 2017, and "The Underhollow", a <a href="/wiki/Battle_royale_game" title="Battle royale game">battle royale</a> mode, in 2018.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Development">Development</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_video_games_derived_from_modifications" class="mw-redirect" title="List of video games derived from modifications">List of video games derived from modifications</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Dota_allstars_game.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/00/Dota_allstars_game.jpg/280px-Dota_allstars_game.jpg" width="280" height="209" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/0/00/Dota_allstars_game.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="365" data-file-height="273" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Dota_allstars_game.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Defense of the Ancients</i>, the original mod from <i>Warcraft III</i> that <i>Dota 2</i> was based on</div></div></div>
<p>The <i>Dota</i> series began in 2003 with <i><a href="/wiki/Defense_of_the_Ancients" title="Defense of the Ancients">Defense of the Ancients</a></i> (<i>DotA</i>)—a mod for <a href="/wiki/Blizzard_Entertainment" title="Blizzard Entertainment">Blizzard Entertainment</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Warcraft_III:_Reign_of_Chaos" title="Warcraft III: Reign of Chaos">Warcraft III: Reign of Chaos</a></i>—created by the pseudonymous designer "Eul".<sup id="cite_ref-gotfragpg1_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gotfragpg1-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> An <a href="/wiki/Expansion_pack" title="Expansion pack">expansion pack</a> for <i>Warcraft III</i>, titled <i><a href="/wiki/The_Frozen_Throne" class="mw-redirect" title="The Frozen Throne">The Frozen Throne</a></i>, was released later that year; a series of <i>Defense of the Ancients</i> <a href="/wiki/Video_game_clone" title="Video game clone">clone mods</a> for the new game competed for popularity. <i>DotA: Allstars</i> by Steve Feak was the most successful,<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> and Feak, with his friend Steve Mescon, created the official <i>Defense of the Ancients</i> community website and the holding company DotA-Allstars, LLC.<sup id="cite_ref-FeakMescon_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FeakMescon-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> When Feak retired from <i>DotA: Allstars</i> in 2005, a friend, under the pseudonym "<a href="/wiki/IceFrog" title="IceFrog">IceFrog</a>", became its lead designer.<sup id="cite_ref-gamasutra-postmortem_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gamasutra-postmortem-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> By the late 2000s, <i>Defense of the Ancients</i> became one of the most popular mods in the world, as well as a prominent <a href="/wiki/Esports" title="Esports">esports</a> title.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> IceFrog and Mescon later had a falling out in May 2009, which prompted the former to establish a new community website at playdota.com.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> Valve's interest in the <i>Defense of the Ancients</i> property began when several veteran employees, including <i><a href="/wiki/Team_Fortress_2" title="Team Fortress 2">Team Fortress 2</a></i> designer <a href="/wiki/Robin_Walker_(game_designer)" title="Robin Walker (game designer)">Robin Walker</a> and executive Erik Johnson, became fans of the mod and wanted to build a modern sequel.<sup id="cite_ref-The_Valve_Way_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Valve_Way-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> The company corresponded with IceFrog by email about his long-term plans for the project,<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> and he was subsequently hired to direct a sequel.<sup id="cite_ref-IGNDOTA2_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IGNDOTA2-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> IceFrog first announced his new position through his blog in October 2009,<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> with <i>Dota 2</i> being publicly announced by <i><a href="/wiki/Game_Informer" title="Game Informer">Game Informer</a></i> a year later.<sup id="cite_ref-ValveDota2_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ValveDota2-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Valve adopted the word "<i>Dota</i>", derived from the original mod's acronym, as the name for its newly acquired franchise. Johnson argued that the word referred to a concept, and was not an acronym.<sup id="cite_ref-IGNDOTA2_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IGNDOTA2-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> Shortly after the announcement of <i>Dota 2</i>, Valve filed a trademark claim to the <i>Dota</i> name.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> At <a href="/wiki/Gamescom" title="Gamescom">Gamescom</a> 2011, company president <a href="/wiki/Gabe_Newell" title="Gabe Newell">Gabe Newell</a> explained that the <a href="/wiki/Trademark" title="Trademark">trademark</a> was needed to develop a sequel with the already-identifiable brand.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> Holding the <i>Dota</i> name to be a community asset, Feak and Mescon filed an opposing trademark for "DOTA" on behalf of DotA-Allstars, LLC (then a subsidiary of <a href="/wiki/Riot_Games" title="Riot Games">Riot Games</a>) in August 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-FeakMescon_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FeakMescon-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Rob_Pardo" title="Rob Pardo">Rob Pardo</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Director_(business)" title="Director (business)">executive vice president</a> of Blizzard Entertainment at the time, similarly stated that the <i>DotA</i> name belonged to the mod's community. Blizzard acquired DotA-Allstars, LLC from Riot Games and filed an opposition against Valve in November 2011, citing Blizzard's ownership of both the <i>Warcraft III World Editor</i> and DotA-Allstars, LLC as proper claims to the franchise.<sup id="cite_ref-BlizzardDOTA_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BlizzardDOTA-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> The dispute was settled in May 2012, with Valve retaining commercial franchising rights to the "<i>Dota</i>" intellectual property, while allowing non-commercial use of the name by third-parties.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> In 2017, Valve's ownership of it was again challenged, after a 2004 <a href="/wiki/Internet_forum" title="Internet forum">internet forum</a> post from Eul was brought to light by a Chinese company known as uCool, who had released a <a href="/wiki/Mobile_game" title="Mobile game">mobile game</a> in 2014 that used characters from the <i>Dota</i> universe.<sup id="cite_ref-uCool_jury_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-uCool_jury-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> uCool, who was previously involved in a lawsuit with Blizzard in 2015 for similar reasons,<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> along with another Chinese company, Lilith Games, argued that the forum post invalidated any ownership claims of the intellectual property, stating that the <i>Dota</i> property was an <a href="/wiki/Open-source_software" title="Open-source software">open-source</a>, <a href="/wiki/Collective_work_(US)" title="Collective work (US)">collective work</a> that could not be copyrighted by anyone in particular.<sup id="cite_ref-uCool_jury_48-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-uCool_jury-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> Judge <a href="/wiki/Charles_R._Breyer" class="mw-redirect" title="Charles R. Breyer">Charles R. Breyer</a> denied uCool's motion for <a href="/wiki/Summary_dismissal" class="mw-redirect" title="Summary dismissal">summary dismissal</a>, but allowed the case to be moved forward to a <a href="/wiki/Jury" title="Jury">jury</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-uCool_jury_48-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-uCool_jury-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>An early goal of the <i>Dota 2</i> team was the adaptation of <i>Defense of the Ancients</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">&#39;s</span> aesthetic style for the <a href="/wiki/Source_(game_engine)" title="Source (game engine)">Source engine</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ValveDota2_43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ValveDota2-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> The Radiant and Dire factions replaced the <a href="/wiki/Night_elves" class="mw-redirect" title="Night elves">Sentinel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Races_and_factions_of_Warcraft#The_Scourge" title="Races and factions of Warcraft">Scourge</a> from the mod, respectively. Character names, abilities, items and map design from the mod were largely retained, with some changes due to trademarks owned by Blizzard. In the first Q&amp;A session regarding <i>Dota 2</i>, IceFrog explained that the game would build upon the mod without making significant changes to its core.<sup id="cite_ref-IGNDOTA2_41-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IGNDOTA2-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> Valve contracted major contributors from the <i>Defense of the Ancients</i> community, including Eul and artist Kendrick Lim, to assist with the sequel.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> Additional contributions from sources outside of Valve were also sought regularly for <i>Dota 2</i>, as to continue <i>Defense of the Ancients</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">&#39;s</span> tradition of community-sourced development.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> One of the composers of <i>Warcraft III: Reign of Chaos</i>, Jason Hayes, was hired to collaborate with <a href="/wiki/Tim_Larkin" title="Tim Larkin">Tim Larkin</a> to write the original score for the game, which was conducted by <a href="/wiki/Timothy_Williams_(composer)" title="Timothy Williams (composer)">Timothy Williams</a> and performed and recorded by the <a href="/wiki/Northwest_Sinfonia" title="Northwest Sinfonia">Northwest Sinfonia</a> at <a href="/wiki/Bastyr_University" title="Bastyr University">Bastyr University</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ComposerHayes_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ComposerHayes-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> Valve had <a href="/wiki/Half-Life_(series)" title="Half-Life (series)"><i>Half-Life</i> series</a> writer <a href="/wiki/Marc_Laidlaw" title="Marc Laidlaw">Marc Laidlaw</a>, science fiction author <a href="/wiki/Ted_Kosmatka" title="Ted Kosmatka">Ted Kosmatka</a>, and Steam support employee Kris Katz write new dialog and background lore for the heroes.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> Notable voice actors for heroes include <a href="/wiki/Nolan_North" title="Nolan North">Nolan North</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dave_Fennoy" title="Dave Fennoy">Dave Fennoy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jon_St._John" title="Jon St. John">Jon St. John</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ellen_McLain" title="Ellen McLain">Ellen McLain</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fred_Tatasciore" title="Fred Tatasciore">Fred Tatasciore</a>, <a href="/wiki/Merle_Dandridge" title="Merle Dandridge">Merle Dandridge</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jen_Taylor" title="Jen Taylor">Jen Taylor</a>, and <a href="/wiki/John_Patrick_Lowrie" title="John Patrick Lowrie">John Patrick Lowrie</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Source engine itself was updated with new features to accommodate <i>Dota 2</i>, such as high-end <a href="/wiki/Cloth_modeling" title="Cloth modeling">cloth modeling</a> and improved global lighting.<sup id="cite_ref-ValveDota2_43-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ValveDota2-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> The game features <a href="/wiki/Steam_(software)" title="Steam (software)">Steam</a> integration, which provides its social component and <a href="/wiki/Cloud_storage" title="Cloud storage">cloud storage</a> for personal settings. In November 2013, Valve introduced a coaching system, which allows experienced players to tutor players with special in-game tools.<sup id="cite_ref-diretide_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-diretide-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> As with previous Valve multiplayer titles, players are able to spectate live matches of <i>Dota 2</i> played by others,<sup id="cite_ref-diretide_59-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-diretide-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Local_area_network" title="Local area network">local area network</a> (LAN) multiplayer support allows for local competitions.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> Some of these events may be spectated via the purchase of tickets from the "Dota Store", which give players in-game access to matches. Ticket fees are apportioned in part to tournament organizers.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> The game also features an in-game <a href="/wiki/Fantasy_sports" class="mw-redirect" title="Fantasy sports">fantasy sports</a> system, which is modeled after traditional fantasy sports and feature professional <i>Dota 2</i> players and teams.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> Players are also able to spectate games in <a href="/wiki/Virtual_reality" title="Virtual reality">virtual reality</a> (VR) with up to 15 others, which was added in an update in July 2016.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> The update also added a hero showcase mode, which allows players to see all of the heroes and their cosmetics full-size in virtual reality.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>As part of a plan to develop <i>Dota 2</i> into a <a href="/wiki/Social_network" title="Social network">social network</a>, Newell announced in April 2012 that the game would be <a href="/wiki/Free-to-play" title="Free-to-play">free-to-play</a>, and that community contributions would be a cornerstone feature.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> Instead, revenue is generated through the "Dota Store", which offers for-purchase cosmetic <a href="/wiki/Virtual_goods" title="Virtual goods">virtual goods</a>, such as custom armor and weapons for their heroes.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> It was also announced that the full roster of heroes would be available at launch for free.<sup id="cite_ref-DotaStore_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DotaStore-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> Until the game's official release in 2013, players were able to purchase an early access bundle, which included a digital copy of <i>Dota 2</i> and several cosmetic items.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> Included as optional <a href="/wiki/Downloadable_content" title="Downloadable content">downloadable content</a> (DLC), the <i>Dota 2</i> Workshop Tools are a set of Source 2 <a href="/wiki/Software_development_kit" title="Software development kit">software development kit</a> (SDK) tools that allow content creators to create new cosmetics for the heroes themselves, as well as custom game modes, maps, and <a href="/wiki/Video_game_bot" title="Video game bot">bot</a> scripts.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> Highly rated cosmetics, through the <a href="/wiki/Steam_Workshop" class="mw-redirect" title="Steam Workshop">Steam Workshop</a>, are available in the in-game store if they are accepted by Valve. This model was fashioned after that of Valve's <i><a href="/wiki/Team_Fortress_2" title="Team Fortress 2">Team Fortress 2</a></i>, which had earned Workshop designers of cosmetic items of that game over $3.5 million by June 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-DotaStore_68-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DotaStore-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> Newell revealed that the average Steam Workshop contributor for <i>Dota 2</i> and <i>Team Fortess 2</i> made approximately $15,000 from their creations in 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> By 2015, sales of <i>Dota 2</i> virtual goods had earned Valve over $238 million in revenue, according to the digital game market research group SuperData.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> In 2016, Valve introduced the "Custom Game Pass" option for creators of custom game modes, which allows them to be funded by way of <a href="/wiki/Microtransaction" title="Microtransaction">microtransactions</a> by adding exclusive features, content, and other changes to their game mode for players who buy it.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><i>Dota 2</i> includes a seasonal <a href="/wiki/Elo_rating_system" title="Elo rating system">Elo rating</a>-based <a href="/wiki/Matchmaking_(video_games)" title="Matchmaking (video games)">matchmaking</a> system, which is measured by a numerical value known as "matchmaking rating" (MMR) and separated into different tiers. MMR is updated based on if a player's team won or lost, which will then <a href="/wiki/Rank_up" title="Rank up">increase or decrease</a> respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-Matchmaking_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Matchmaking-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> The game's servers, known as the "Game Coordinator",<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> attempts to balance both teams based on each player's MMR, with each team having roughly a 50% chance to win in any given game.<sup id="cite_ref-Matchmaking_76-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Matchmaking-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> Ranked game modes with a separately tracked MMR are also available, which primarily differ from unranked games by making MMR publicly visible, as well as requiring the registration of a <a href="/wiki/Phone_number" class="mw-redirect" title="Phone number">phone number</a> to their accounts, which help foster a more competitive environment.<sup id="cite_ref-Matchmaking_76-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Matchmaking-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-MMchanges2017_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MMchanges2017-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> To ensure that each player's ranking is up to date and accurate, MMR is recalibrated around every six months.<sup id="cite_ref-Seasonal_MMR_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Seasonal_MMR-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-IGN_MMR_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IGN_MMR-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> Players with the highest possible medal rank are listed by Valve on an online <a href="/wiki/Leaderboard" class="mw-redirect" title="Leaderboard">leaderboard</a>, separated into North American, European, Southeast Asian, and Chinese regions.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> The game also includes a report system, which allows players to punish player behavior that <a href="/wiki/Griefer" title="Griefer">intentionally provides a negative experience</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> Players who get reported enough or leave a number of games before they have finished, a practice known in-game as "abandoning", are then placed into "low priority" matchmaking, which remains on a player's account until they win a specific number of games, and only groups them with other players who also have the same punishment.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> Other features include an improved replay system from <i>Defense of the Ancients</i>, in which a completed game can be downloaded in-client and viewed by anyone at a later time, and the "hero builds" feature, which provide integrated guides created by the community that highlight to the player on how to play their hero.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dota_2_Reborn"><i>Dota 2 Reborn</i></span></h3>
<p>In June 2015, Valve announced that the entirety of <i>Dota 2</i> would be ported over to their <a href="/wiki/Source_2" class="mw-redirect" title="Source 2">Source 2</a> game engine in an update called <i>Dota 2 Reborn</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> <i>Reborn</i> was first released to the public as an opt-in beta update that same month,<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> and officially replaced the original client in September 2015, making it the first game to use the engine.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> <i>Reborn</i> included a new user interface framework design, ability for custom game modes created by the community, and the full replacement of the original Source engine with Source 2.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> Largely attributed to technical difficulties players experienced with the update, the global player base experienced a sharp drop of approximately sixteen percent the month following the release of it.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> However, after various updates and <a href="/wiki/Patch_(computing)" title="Patch (computing)">patches</a>, over a million concurrent players were playing again by the beginning of 2016, with that number being the largest in nearly a year.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> The move to Source 2 also allowed the use of the <a href="/wiki/Vulkan_(API)" title="Vulkan (API)">Vulkan graphics API</a>, which was released as an optional feature in May 2016, making <i>Dota 2</i> one of the first games to offer it.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Release">Release</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_International_Dota_2_video_game_tournament.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/The_International_Dota_2_video_game_tournament.jpg/300px-The_International_Dota_2_video_game_tournament.jpg" width="300" height="200" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/The_International_Dota_2_video_game_tournament.jpg/450px-The_International_Dota_2_video_game_tournament.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/The_International_Dota_2_video_game_tournament.jpg/600px-The_International_Dota_2_video_game_tournament.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1920" data-file-height="1280" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_International_Dota_2_video_game_tournament.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Gamescom" title="Gamescom">Gamescom</a> 2011 in <a href="/wiki/Cologne" title="Cologne">Cologne</a>, where the game was first made playable to the public</div></div></div>
<p><i>Dota 2</i> was first made available to the public at <a href="/wiki/Gamescom" title="Gamescom">Gamescom</a> in 2011, coinciding with the inaugural <a href="/wiki/The_International_2011" class="mw-redirect" title="The International 2011">International</a> championship, the game's premier <a href="/wiki/Esport" class="mw-redirect" title="Esport">esport</a> tournament event. At the event, Valve began sending out <a href="/wiki/Beta_release" class="mw-redirect" title="Beta release">closed beta</a> invitations, with the first few being sent out shortly after Gamescom.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> During the event, Newell speculated that <i>Dota 2</i> would likely ship in 2012, despite original plans for a full release in late 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-Dota2012_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dota2012-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> In September 2011, Valve scrapped its previous development and release plans, which would have kept the game in its closed beta phase for over a year. IceFrog then announced plans to begin beta testing. Simultaneously, Valve announced that the <a href="/wiki/Non-disclosure_agreement" title="Non-disclosure agreement">non-disclosure agreement</a> for the beta was being lifted, allowing testers to discuss the game and their experiences publicly.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>After nearly two years of beta testing, <i>Dota 2</i> was officially released on <a href="/wiki/Steam_(software)" title="Steam (software)">Steam</a> for <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Windows" title="Microsoft Windows">Microsoft Windows</a> on July 9, 2013, and later for <a href="/wiki/OS_X" class="mw-redirect" title="OS X">OS X</a> and <a href="/wiki/Linux" title="Linux">Linux</a> on July 18, 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> The game did not launch with every hero from <i>Defense of the Ancients</i>. Instead, the missing ones were added in various post-release updates, with the final one, as well as the first <i>Dota 2</i> original hero, being added in 2016.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> Two months following the game's release, Newell claimed that updates to <i>Dota 2</i> generated up to three percent of global internet traffic.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> In December 2013, the final restrictions against unlimited global access to <i>Dota 2</i> were lifted after the game's infrastructure and servers were substantially bolstered.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> In order to abide by the standards set by the economic legislation of specific countries, Valve opted to contract with nationally based developers for publishing. In October 2012, Chinese game publisher, <a href="/wiki/Perfect_World_(company)" class="mw-redirect" title="Perfect World (company)">Perfect World</a>, announced their acquisition for distribution rights of the game in the country.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> The Chinese version also has a region-specific "Low Violence" mode, which censors and changes most depictions of blood, gore, and skulls in order for the game to follow <a href="/wiki/Censorship_in_China" title="Censorship in China">censorship policies of the country</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> In November 2012, a similar publishing deal was made with the South Korea-based game company <a href="/wiki/Nexon" title="Nexon">Nexon</a> to distribute and market the game in the country, as well as in Japan.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> Three years later, Nexon announced they would no longer be operating servers for <i>Dota 2</i>, with Valve taking over direct distribution and marketing of the game in those regions.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In December 2016, <i>Dota 2</i> was updated to <a href="/wiki/Software_versioning" title="Software versioning">gameplay version</a> 7.00, also known as "The New Journey" update.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-7.00_GameSpot_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7.00_GameSpot-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-7.00_PC_Gamer_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7.00_PC_Gamer-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> Prior to the update, the <i>Dota</i> series had been in version 6.<i>xx</i> for over a decade, marking the first major revision since IceFrog originally took over development of the original mod in the mid 2000s.<sup id="cite_ref-7.00_PC_Gamer_109-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7.00_PC_Gamer-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> The New Journey update added and changed numerous features and mechanics of the game, including adding the first original hero not ported over from <i>Defense of the Ancients</i>, a reworked map, a redesigned <a href="/wiki/HUD_(video_gaming)" class="mw-redirect" title="HUD (video gaming)">HUD</a>, a pre-game phase that allows for players to discuss their team strategy, and a "Talent Tree" hero augmentation system.<sup id="cite_ref-7.00_GameSpot_108-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7.00_GameSpot-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-7.00_PC_Gamer_109-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7.00_PC_Gamer-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> In April 2017, Valve announced changes to the game's ranked matchmaking system, with the main one requiring the registration of a unique <a href="/wiki/Phone_number" class="mw-redirect" title="Phone number">phone number</a> to a player's account in order to play them, an <a href="/wiki/Griefer" title="Griefer">anti-griefing</a> and <a href="/wiki/Smurfing_(video_games)" class="mw-redirect" title="Smurfing (video games)">smurfing</a> practice they had previously implemented in their <a href="/wiki/First-person_shooter" title="First-person shooter">first-person shooter</a> game, <i><a href="/wiki/Counter-Strike:_Global_Offensive" title="Counter-Strike: Global Offensive">Counter-Strike: Global Offensive</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-MMchanges2017_78-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MMchanges2017-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> Further changes to the game's matchmaking were brought in an update in November 2017, where the old numerical MMR system was replaced by a seasonal one based on eight ranked "medals" that are recalibrated every six months, a move that brought the game's ranked system closer to ones used in other competitive games such as <i>Global Offensive</i>, <i><a href="/wiki/StarCraft_(video_game)" title="StarCraft (video game)">StarCraft</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/League_of_Legends" title="League of Legends">League of Legends</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In early 2018, IceFrog announced that he and Valve would begin on a different way to handle <a href="/wiki/Balance_(game_design)" class="mw-redirect" title="Balance (game design)">gameplay balance</a> updates for the game. Instead of releasing larger updates irregularly throughout the year, smaller ones would be released on a set schedule of every two weeks.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> Around the same time, the game also introduced the "Dota Plus" paid monthly subscription system that replaced the individual <a href="/wiki/Battle_pass" title="Battle pass">battle passes</a> that were released to coincide with that season's Major tournament. In addition to offering what they previously did, Dota Plus added new features such as a hero-specific achievement system that reward players who complete them with exclusive cosmetics, as well as providing hero and game analytics and statistics gathered from thousands of recent games.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Professional_competition">Professional competition</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_International_2018_(43467814784).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/The_International_2018_%2843467814784%29.jpg/300px-The_International_2018_%2843467814784%29.jpg" width="300" height="200" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/The_International_2018_%2843467814784%29.jpg/450px-The_International_2018_%2843467814784%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/The_International_2018_%2843467814784%29.jpg/600px-The_International_2018_%2843467814784%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5674" data-file-height="3783" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_International_2018_(43467814784).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The largest <i>Dota 2</i> tournaments often have prize pools totaling millions of dollars. Shown here is <a href="/wiki/The_International_2018" title="The International 2018">The International 2018</a>, which was a $25 million tournament hosted at the <a href="/wiki/Rogers_Arena" title="Rogers Arena">Rogers Arena</a> in Vancouver.</div></div></div>
<p>To ensure that enough <i>Defense of the Ancients</i> players would take up <i>Dota 2</i> and to promote the game to a new audience, Valve invited sixteen accomplished <i>Defense of the Ancients</i> <a href="/wiki/Esports" title="Esports">esports</a> teams to compete at a <i>Dota 2</i>-specific tournament at <a href="/wiki/Gamescom" title="Gamescom">Gamescom</a> in August 2011, which later became an annually held event known as <a href="/wiki/The_International_(Dota_2)" title="The International (Dota 2)">The International</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> From <a href="/wiki/The_International_2013" class="mw-redirect" title="The International 2013">The International 2013</a> onward, its prize pool was allowed to be <a href="/wiki/Crowdfunded" class="mw-redirect" title="Crowdfunded">crowdfunded</a> through a type of optional in-game <a href="/wiki/Battle_pass" title="Battle pass">battle pass</a> called the "Compendium", which raises money from players buying them and connected <a href="/wiki/Lootboxes" class="mw-redirect" title="Lootboxes">lootboxes</a> to get exclusive in-game cosmetics and other bonuses offered through them.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Compendium_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Compendium-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> 25% of all the revenue made from Compendiums go directly to the prize pool, with sales from the 2013 battle pass raising over <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$</span>2.8 million, which made it the largest prize pool in esports history at the time.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> Each iteration of The International since then has surpassed the previous one's prize pool, with the most recent one, <a href="/wiki/The_International_2018" title="The International 2018">The International 2018</a>, having one at over $25 million.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>During its beta phase in the early 2010s, several other esport events would begin hosting <i>Dota 2</i> events, including the <a href="/wiki/Electronic_Sports_World_Cup" title="Electronic Sports World Cup">Electronic Sports World Cup</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/DreamHack" title="DreamHack">DreamHack</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/World_Cyber_Games" title="World Cyber Games">World Cyber Games</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/ESL_(company)" title="ESL (company)">ESL</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> By the end of 2011, <i>Dota 2</i> was already one of the highest-paying esport games, second only to <i><a href="/wiki/StarCraft_II" class="mw-redirect" title="StarCraft II">StarCraft II</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> At <a href="/wiki/E3_2013" class="mw-redirect" title="E3 2013">E3 2013</a>, South Korean company <a href="/wiki/Nexon" title="Nexon">Nexon</a> announced the investment of <a href="/wiki/South_Korean_won" title="South Korean won">₩</a>2 billion (approximately <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$</span>1.7 million) into local leagues in the country, which coincided with their distribution partnership with Valve for the game.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> In February 2015, Valve sponsored <a href="/wiki/Dota_2_Asia_Championships" class="mw-redirect" title="Dota 2 Asia Championships"><i>Dota 2</i> Asia Championships</a> was held in <a href="/wiki/Shanghai" title="Shanghai">Shanghai</a> with a prize pool of over $3 million, raised through compendium sales.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> Since then, other <i>Dota 2</i> Asia Championships have taken place, with it being sometimes being referred to as the "Chinese International".<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> In total, professional <i>Dota 2</i> tournaments had earned teams and players over $100 million by June 2017, with over half of that being awarded at the International tournaments, making it the highest earning esport game by a margin of nearly $60 million.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303569">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti>.thumbinner{width:100%!important;max-width:none!important}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:none!important;width:100%!important;text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:508px;max-width:508px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:252px;max-width:252px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:The_International_2018_(30302304258).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/The_International_2018_%2830302304258%29.jpg/250px-The_International_2018_%2830302304258%29.jpg" width="250" height="167" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/The_International_2018_%2830302304258%29.jpg/375px-The_International_2018_%2830302304258%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/The_International_2018_%2830302304258%29.jpg/500px-The_International_2018_%2830302304258%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="6720" data-file-height="4480" /></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:252px;max-width:252px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:The_International_2018_(30302303908).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/The_International_2018_%2830302303908%29.jpg/250px-The_International_2018_%2830302303908%29.jpg" width="250" height="167" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/The_International_2018_%2830302303908%29.jpg/375px-The_International_2018_%2830302303908%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/The_International_2018_%2830302303908%29.jpg/500px-The_International_2018_%2830302303908%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5862" data-file-height="3908" /></a></div></div><div style="clear:left"></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent">As with traditional sporting events, most major <i>Dota 2</i> events feature pre- and post-game discussion by a panel of analysts (<i>left</i>), with in-match casting being done by <a href="/wiki/Play-by-play" class="mw-redirect" title="Play-by-play">play-by-play</a> and <a href="/wiki/Color_commentators" class="mw-redirect" title="Color commentators">color commentators</a> (<i>right</i>).</div></div></div>
<p>From late 2015 until early 2017, Valve sponsored a series of smaller-scale, seasonally held tournaments known as the Dota Major Championships, which each had fixed prize pools of <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$</span>3 million.<sup id="cite_ref-Majors_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Majors-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> Their format was based on the <a href="/wiki/Counter-Strike:_Global_Offensive_Majors" class="mw-redirect" title="Counter-Strike: Global Offensive Majors">tournament series of the same name</a> that Valve also sponsored for their <a href="/wiki/First-person_shooter" title="First-person shooter">first-person shooter</a> game, <i><a href="/wiki/Counter-Strike:_Global_Offensive" title="Counter-Strike: Global Offensive">Counter-Strike: Global Offensive</a></i>. Including <a href="/wiki/The_International_2016" title="The International 2016">The International 2016</a> and <a href="/wiki/The_International_2017" title="The International 2017">2017</a>, which were considered to be the cumulative Major of their respective seasons,<sup id="cite_ref-Majors_134-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Majors-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> the series had five other events, which were the <a href="/wiki/Frankfurt_Major" title="Frankfurt Major">Frankfurt Major</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Shanghai_Major" title="Shanghai Major">Shanghai Major</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Manila_Major" title="Manila Major">Manila Major</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Boston_Major" title="Boston Major">Boston Major</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Kiev_Major" title="Kiev Major">Kiev Major</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> Following the International 2017, the Majors were replaced with the <a href="/wiki/Dota_Pro_Circuit" title="Dota Pro Circuit">Dota Pro Circuit</a> (DPC) format due to criticism by teams and fans for Valve's non-transparent and unpredictable nature for handing out International invitations.<sup id="cite_ref-Majors/Minors_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Majors/Minors-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Majors/Minors_PC_Gamer_143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Majors/Minors_PC_Gamer-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Majors/Minors_Kotaku_144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Majors/Minors_Kotaku-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> In the DPC, teams are awarded qualification points for their performance in sponsored tournaments, with the top twelve earning direct invites to that season's International.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> To avoid conflicting dates with other tournaments, Valve directly manages the scheduling of them.<sup id="cite_ref-Majors/Minors_Kotaku_144-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Majors/Minors_Kotaku-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The primary <a href="/wiki/Media_(communication)" title="Media (communication)">medium</a> for professional <i>Dota 2</i> coverage is through the <a href="/wiki/Video_game_live_streaming" title="Video game live streaming">video game live streaming</a> platform, <a href="/wiki/Twitch.tv" title="Twitch.tv">Twitch.tv</a>. For most major events, tournament coverage is done by a selection of dedicated esports organizations and personnel who provide on-site <a href="/wiki/Sports_commentator" title="Sports commentator">commentary</a>, analysis, match predictions, and player interviews surrounding the event in progress, similar to traditional sporting events.<sup id="cite_ref-1mil_146-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1mil-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup> Live <i>Dota 2</i> games and coverage have also been <a href="/wiki/Simulcast" title="Simulcast">simulcast</a> on television networks around the world, such as <a href="/wiki/ESPN" title="ESPN">ESPN</a> in the United States,<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/BBC_Three_(online)" title="BBC Three (online)">BBC Three</a> in the United Kingdom,<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sport1_(Germany)" title="Sport1 (Germany)">Sport1</a> in Germany,<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/TV_2_Zulu" title="TV 2 Zulu">TV 2 Zulu</a> in Denmark,<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Xinwen_Lianbo" title="Xinwen Lianbo">Xinwen Lianbo</a> in China,<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Astro_(television)" title="Astro (television)">Astro</a> in Malaysia,<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/TV5_(Philippines)" class="mw-redirect" title="TV5 (Philippines)">TV5</a> in the Philippines.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Reception">Reception</span></h2>
